    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="444aa6778fd24c50832077bd16f61038">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '2723806b-b477-4dea-af39-0f9bc1d487e8'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

class="w-slide__hide" href="#aenm202405706-sec-0080-title"><span> Acknowledgements</span></a></li><li><a class="w-slide__hide" href="#aenm202405706-sec-0090-title"><span> Conflict of    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d177245293" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Heterostructure engineering and active component reconstruction are effective strategies for efficient and rapid charge storage in advanced sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Herein, sandwich-type CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene composites are used as a model to reconstruct new active Cu<sub>2</sub>Se@MXene heterostructures by in situ electrochemical driving. The MXene core provides interconnected pathways for electron and ion conduction, while also buffering volumetric expansion to stabilize the structure. This reconstructed Cu<sub>2</sub>Se@MXene heterointerface features abundant sodium storage active sites, enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption, and diffusion kinetics, thus increasing sodium storage capacity. Moreover, the elevated Co valence state during the discharge process allows it to act as an electron reservoir to provide additional electron supply for Cu<sub>2</sub>Se conversion and accelerate the sodium storage kinetics. When employed as an anode in SIBs, the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode exhibits high capacity (694 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>), excellent rate performance (425 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>), and exceptional durability (437 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 10&nbsp;000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a 0.0014% capacity decay per cycle). The electrochemical reconstruction and sodium storage mechanism of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se@MXene anode is further revealed through ex situ characterization and theoretical calculations. This work provides a new approach for designing advanced conversion-type anodes for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405706-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405706-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In recent years, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as a viable alternative and complementary technology to lithium-ion batteries and have experienced rapid development. The unique competitive advantages of SIBs can be attributed to their inherent properties. Firstly, the higher electrostatic energy of sodium salts compared to lithium salts, such as NaPF<sub>6</sub> vs LiPF<sub>6</sub>, leads to enhanced thermal stability, which holds promise for safety and excellent Na storage performance in high-temperature environments.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0001_R_d177245273e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Rev.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The desolvation energy of Na<sup>+</sup> in SIBs is ≈25–30% lower than that of Li<sup>+</sup>, which reduces the activation barrier of the de-/sodiation process and significantly improves the electrode kinetics, allowing SIBs to exhibit outstanding performance even in extremely cold environments.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0002_R_d177245273e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, SIBs can be transported and stored at 0 V, endowing them with higher abuse tolerance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0003_R_d177245273e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, SIBs are less susceptible to safety hazards such as thermal runaway or explosion under extreme conditions such as overcharging or overheating, demonstrating enhanced stability and safety. Despite the above advantages, the larger Na⁺ radius (1.02 Å vs 0.76 Å for Li⁺) hinders its insertion in the anode (especially at high rates), resulting in sluggish Na⁺ diffusion kinetics and significant volume changes during sodiation/desodiation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0004_R_d177245273e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a>-<a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0006_R_d177245273e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - eScience.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, constructing stable structures with fast sodium storage behavior is crucial for the development of SIBs with high-rate capability and long-term cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p>Transition metal selenides (TMSe) have attracted attention as anodes for SIBs in recent years due to their high capacity based on multi-step conversion reactions, excellent electrical conductivity (1 × 10<sup>−3</sup> S m<sup>−1</sup> for Se vs 5 × 10<sup>−28</sup> S m<sup>−1</sup> for S), and superior rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0007_R_d177245273e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the larger Se atomic radius weakens the M-Se bond making it relatively easy to break compared to its M–O and M–S counterparts, thus favoring the rapid conversion reaction kinetics of TMSe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0008_R_d177245273e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite these advantages, TMSe still faces issues such as significant volume expansion during sodiation/desodiation and sluggish reaction kinetics, which result in poor rate performance and cycling stability. The most common strategy to address these challenges is to design and fabricate conductive carbon-based substrates to support nanostructured TMSe in forming heterostructures, thereby enhancing the conductivity for ion/electron transfer and providing sufficient space to buffer volume changes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0009_R_d177245273e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Matter.">9</a>-<a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0012_R_d177245273e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the limited conjugate planar positions of nonpolar carbon matrix restrict its fixation and capture ability for polar TMSe. Weak interfacial electric/chemical coupling further hinders charge transfer and forms voltage hysteresis during cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0013_R_d177245273e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">13</a>, <a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0014_R_d177245273e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, new strategies need to be explored to design TMSe-based heterostructures that exhibit ultrafast charge transfer and interfacial electron interactions. Recently, transition metal carbides or carbonitrides, known as MXenes, have emerged as a new class of 2D materials with advantages such as tunable layered structures, metallic conductivity, and high mechanical stability, attracting significant attention in the field of electrochemical energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0015_R_d177245273e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano.">15</a>-<a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0017_R_d177245273e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Most importantly, the etching process of MXenes introduces abundant terminal groups on their surfaces, along with strong polarity and excellent ability to regulate charge distribution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0018_R_d177245273e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Science.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0019_R_d177245273e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nat. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These advantages make it possible to offer multiple possibilities for the design of TMSe-based heterostructures, which in turn optimize the electronic and interfacial properties of TMSe and improve charge transfer kinetics and cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p>In addition to strategies such as nanostructure design, the combination of carbon substrates, and the construction of heterostructures, electrolyte optimization is also an effective strategy to enhance the electrochemical performance of TMSe. Gong et&nbsp;al. found that using ether-based electrolytes instead of carbonate-based electrolytes can reduce the side reactions of electrodes, promote the formation of thin and stable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI), and achieve higher Coulombic efficiency and better cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0020_R_d177245273e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Lett.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although most studies have revealed the mechanisms and advantages of ether-based electrolytes, the influence of the current collector, specifically copper (Cu), has been neglected. Li et&nbsp;al. first proposed a Cu-driven conversion mechanism under ether-based electrolytes, in which Cu foil reacts with the discharge product Na<sub>2</sub>Se during the initial cycles to form Cu<sub>2</sub>Se, which then serves as the active material for subsequent cycles. In contrast, the Cu-driven conversion process was not detected in ester-based electrolytes, and it was accompanied by a rapid decline in capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0021_R_d177245273e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Xu et&nbsp;al. further elucidated the driving force behind this electrochemical transformation using the “hard-soft-acid-base (HSAB) theory”. In this process, Cu from the Cu foil gradually infiltrates into the electrode and competes with metal ions in MSe<sub>x</sub> to form Cu<sub>2</sub>Se.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0022_R_d177245273e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cu<sub>2</sub>Se, as a superionic conductor, exhibits high ionic mobility while possessing a unique sodium storage mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0007_R_d177245273e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Xu et&nbsp;al. then synergistically achieved superior sodium storage in TMSe by constructing N-doped carbon foam and utilizing Cu<sub>2</sub>Se generated through Cu-driven conversion reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0023_R_d177245273e657" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Small.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In our previous research on Na-Se batteries, we also discovered the Cu conversion reaction, which facilitated the adsorption and rapid conversion of polyselenides to enhance sodium storage performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0024_R_d177245273e663" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, this Cu-driven conversion mechanism also occurs in transition metal sulfides and has been utilized to improve sodium storage performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0025_R_d177245273e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Mater.">25</a>-<a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0028_R_d177245273e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the Cu-driven conversion mechanism in ether electrolytes can have a beneficial effect on sodium storage in TMSe. Meanwhile, the potential mechanism of its enhanced electrochemical performance and sodium storage behavior needs to be further explored.</p>
         
         <p>Inspired by the above analysis, herein, we have designed sandwich-type CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene heterostructures by a facile solvothermal process and selenisation strategy. Meanwhile, the Cu-driven conversion reaction makes CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene as the anode of SIBs exhibits high reversible specific capacity (694 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>), superb rate capability (425 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and outstanding cycle stability (437 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 10&nbsp;000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of ∼86%). The excellent sodium storage performance can be attributed to the following factors: i) The two-dimensional structure of MXene effectively refines and disperses the selenide nanoparticles, ensuring better interfacial contact and high conversion reversibility. ii) The conductive MXene substrate forms a robust conductive pathway for electron/ion transfer, buffering volume expansion and maintaining the structural stability of the electrode material. iii) The CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene heterojunction facilitates Na adsorption and provides richer interfacial active sites. iv) The in-situ reconstituted Cu<sub>2</sub>Se serves as new active sites with enhanced sodium storage performance, exhibiting stronger Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energy and diffusion kinetics. v) The displaced Co element can act as an electron collector, providing and collecting additional electrons for the conversion reaction of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se, effectively promoting sodium storage. A series of ex situ characterizations and theoretical calculations were conducted to further elucidate the superior Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance and storage mechanism. We believe that the design strategy of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene heterostructure and the Cu-driven conversion mechanism can also be extended to other TMSe. This work will provide insights for a deeper understanding of Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms and the development of composites for SIBs with high rate capability and long cycling life.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405706-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405706-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405706-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405706-sec-0030-title">2.1 Material Synthesis and Characterizations</h3>
            
            <p>The synthesis process of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene is illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0001">1a</a>. First, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene nanosheets were prepared via etching and delamination processes of the Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> MAX phase. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0001">1b</a>, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene presents uniform nanosheet morphology with a smooth surface. Its 2D structure and surface negative charge properties favor the adsorption of cobalt cations and subsequent in situ crystal growth. Then, CoS particles were formed by in situ coupling of Co and S sources on Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene sheets (CoS@MXene). CoS adheres to the MXene surface as fine particles of ≈20 nm size (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0001">1c</a>). Finally, the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene composite was synthesized through the conversion of the CoS@MXene under a selenization process by the thermal evaporation of selenium powders in a tube furnace. The CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene composite retains the same sandwich structure as CoS@MXene (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0001">1d</a>). Furthermore, the TEM images of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0001">1e,f</a>) exhibited that CoSe<sub>2</sub> particles with an average size of 18.6 ± 3.4 nm were uniformly dispersed on the MXene surface. As shown in the HRTEM image of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0001">1g</a>, the clear fringe spacings of 0.29 nm were well-matched with (101) planes of orthorhombic CoSe<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No. 53–0449), indicating the successful synthesis of CoSe<sub>2</sub>. The selected area electron diffraction pattern is clearly presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), where four diffraction rings marked with green color are matched to the (230), (211), (101), and (111) planes of CoSe<sub>2</sub>, aligning with the findings in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0001">1g</a>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0001">1h</a>, the TEM and elemental mapping images reveal that Co, Se, S, Ti, and C elements were uniformly distributed over the entire CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene composite. Specifically, although a small amount of S remains, the atomic ratio of Co to Se ≈ 1:2 confirms the elemental composition of CoSe<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). In order to demonstrate the advantages of MXene architecture, pure CoSe<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles were also prepared for comparison. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), pure CoSe<sub>2</sub> particles aggregate in clusters with an average size of 59.4 ± 14.9 nm. In contrast, CoSe<sub>2</sub> in the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene composite exhibits smaller particle sizes, indicating that the introduction of MXene can inhibit grain growth and thus refine the particles. Such tiny nanoparticles could efficiently accommodate the internal stress generated during the sodiation and desodiation processes and shorten the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405706-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1d08a331-a9b6-4c95-9fad-a92e8d598a40/aenm202405706-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1d08a331-a9b6-4c95-9fad-a92e8d598a40/aenm202405706-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b43c37ac-c870-4713-9642-f4ece35ed841/aenm202405706-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1d08a331-a9b6-4c95-9fad-a92e8d598a40/aenm202405706-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405706-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration for the synthesis of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene. b) TEM image of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene. SEM images of c) CoS@MXene and d) CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene. e,f) TEM and g) HRTEM images of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene, the inset in f) is particle size histograms. h) TEM image and elemental mapping images of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0002">2a</a> shows the XRD patterns of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene and pure CoSe<sub>2</sub>, where the diffraction peaks of both samples located at 29.0°, 30.8°, 34.5°, 35.9°, 47.7°, 50.2°, 53.5°, 56.9°, and 63.3° can be well assigned to the (011), (101), (111), (120), (211), (002), (031), (131), and (122) planes of CoSe<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No. 53–0449). Specifically, the XRD pattern of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene composite does not detect the (002) diffraction peak of MXene between 5° and 10°, which is due to the signal of the inner low-content MXene being blocked by the outer-coated CoSe<sub>2</sub> particles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0029_R_d177245273e872" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the diffraction peaks of the intermediate phase CoS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) were not detected in the composites, indicating the successful preparation of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene. The TG analysis in the air atmosphere was performed to determine the weight percentage of CoSe<sub>2</sub> in the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene composite. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), the weight change of the composite mainly stems from the oxidation of MXene and CoSe<sub>2</sub> (Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> + 5O<sub>2</sub> → 3TiO<sub>2</sub> + 2CO<sub>2</sub>, 3CoSe<sub>2</sub> + 8O<sub>2</sub> → Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> + 6SeO<sub>2</sub>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0030_R_d177245273e914" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">30</a>-<a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0032_R_d177245273e914" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After experiencing weight gain and loss during oxidation, respectively, only TiO<sub>2</sub> and Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (47.8 wt%) remained in the sample at 600 °C. Accordingly, the content of CoSe<sub>2</sub> in the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene composite can be estimated as 87.9% according to the TGA results. In addition, the specific surface area (SSA) and porosity were studied through N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption measurements. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0002">2b</a>, the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene composite displayed Type III isotherm with an Type H3 hysteresis loop, suggesting the existence of mesopores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0033_R_d177245273e938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Nano.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene delivered a Brunauer-Emmett-Teller surface area of 20.5 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> with the pore sizes mainly at 2.0, 2.7, and 3.6 nm. Such appropriate SSA and the mesoporous microstructure would be beneficial to facilitating electrolyte penetration and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405706-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a10221a0-2faf-44ff-952c-173eccc299a6/aenm202405706-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a10221a0-2faf-44ff-952c-173eccc299a6/aenm202405706-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7846d2bb-f8fc-42ed-b183-a0d96682f7ac/aenm202405706-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a10221a0-2faf-44ff-952c-173eccc299a6/aenm202405706-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405706-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene and pure CoSe<sub>2</sub>. b) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherm and pore size distribution curves (inset) of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene. High-resolution XPS spectra of c) Co 2p, d) Se 3d, and e) S 2p-Se3p of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene and pure CoSe<sub>2</sub>. f) Ti 2p XPS spectrum of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The surface chemical states of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene and pure CoSe<sub>2</sub> were investigated by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), as shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0002">2c-f</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>. In high-resolution Co 2p spectra, the peaks at 778.4 eV (793.4 eV), 780.1 eV (795.9 eV), and 783.5 eV (802.1 eV) were assigned to the Co-Se bonds, Co<sup>2+</sup>-O bonds, and satellite peaks, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0002">2c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0015_R_d177245273e1005" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano.">15</a>, <a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0034_R_d177245273e1005" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Nano.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Se 3d spectra, the peaks at 54.5, 55.4, and 59.3 eV were related to the Co-Se, Se-Se, and Se-O bonds, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0002">2d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0031_R_d177245273e1013" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Mater.">31</a>, <a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0032_R_d177245273e1013" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For S spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0002">2e</a>), it can be deconvoluted into four peaks due to the overlap of S 2p and Se 3p spectra. The peaks centered at 161.0 and 166.4 eV were indexed to the Se 3p<sub>3/2</sub> and Se 3p<sub>1/2</sub>, while the other pair of smaller peaks at 163.9 and 165.1 eV correspond to the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively, indicating the partial substitution of Se by S in the form of Co-S bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0035_R_d177245273e1031" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Small.">35</a>, <a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0036_R_d177245273e1031" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Small Methods.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the Ti 2p spectrum of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0002">2f</a>), four predominant Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peaks at 455.2, 455.9, 456.6, and 458.4 eV correspond to Ti-C, Ti(II)-O, Ti(III)-O, and Ti(IV)-O, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0037_R_d177245273e1043" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">37</a>, <a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0038_R_d177245273e1043" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, a new weak signal appears at 471.3 eV, which may be due to the formation of a heterostructure between CoSe<sub>2</sub> and MXene resulting in a new outer electron distribution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0032_R_d177245273e1051" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405706-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405706-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Evaluation for SIBs</h3>
            
            <p>The sodium storage properties of as-prepared samples were evaluated in a CR2032 coin-type cell using a metal sodium as the counter electrode and 1.0 M NaSO<sub>3</sub>CF<sub>3</sub> in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether as the electrolyte. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0003">3a</a> shows the initial five cyclic voltammograms (CV) curves of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode collected at 0.2 mV s<sup>−1</sup> within the voltage range of 0.3-3.0 V. In the first cathodic scan, the reduction peak at 0.92 V was attributed to the sodiation process of CoSe<sub>2</sub> into Na<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>2</sub>/Na<sub>2</sub>Se/Co and the formation of a stable SEI film.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0023_R_d177245273e1088" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Small.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Afterwards, the two oxidation peaks at 1.81 and 1.91 V correspond to the multi-step desodiation processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0013_R_d177245273e1093" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> From the second scan, the cathodic peaks appeared at 1.44 and 1.09 V deliver the typical sodiation behavior similar to that of cobalt selenide based anode. In addition, the changes in current intensity at the anodic peaks (1.81 and 1.91 V) belong to the activation process of the electrode. Interestingly, the appearance and enhancement of the peaks at 0.67 V (cathodic scan) as well as at 1.52 V (anodic scan) are related to the sodiation/desodiation process of self-derived Cu<sub>2</sub>Se new phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0023_R_d177245273e1100" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Small.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The generation of this new phase will be validated in subsequent ex-situ XRD tests. The electrochemical activation process and structural reorganization of the electrode are also reflected in the galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD) profiles in the initial five cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0003">3b</a>. The working potential platforms were well matched with the peaks in CV curves. Specifically, as the charge and discharge proceed, a gradually broadened charging potential platform appears at 1.50 V, corresponding to the anodic peak (1.52 V) observed in the CV curve. Clearly, the initial discharge and charge-specific capacities were 646 and 602 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, giving an ultrahigh initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 93%. By contrast, the pure CoSe<sub>2</sub> and the CoS@MXene electrodes deliver an inferior ICE of 88% and 91%, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The GCD curves of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene, pure CoSe<sub>2</sub>, and CoS@MXene electrodes at various current densities from 0.1 to 20 A g<sup>−1</sup> were presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information). All GCD curves show clearly visible plateaus. In comparison, the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode can maintain distinct voltage plateaus even at high current density, demonstrating its excellent rate capability and rapid sodiation/desodiation kinetics. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0003">3c</a>, the prepared CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode delivers high specific capacities of 648, 643, 625, 614, 584, 538, 511, and 425 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, and 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Even when the current density returns to the initial 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, an average reversible capacity of 686 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can still be achieved for the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode, which is 1.1 and 1.4 times higher than that of pure CoSe<sub>2</sub> electrode (625 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and CoS@MXene electrode (491 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), respectively. In particular, the specific capacity of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode at a high current density of 20 A g<sup>−1</sup> was even 3.0 and 11.4 times higher than that of pure CoSe<sub>2</sub> electrode (142 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and CoS@MXene electrode (37 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), respectively. For pure MXene (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), the specific capacity was 109 and 6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 and 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Among all the electrodes, the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode exhibits the best rate performance and higher sodium storage capacity.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405706-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/610bd6fa-29cf-4e12-a0fc-76479ac7ba50/aenm202405706-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/610bd6fa-29cf-4e12-a0fc-76479ac7ba50/aenm202405706-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3b253e12-1323-45e4-8f63-fd0d89cf7184/aenm202405706-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/610bd6fa-29cf-4e12-a0fc-76479ac7ba50/aenm202405706-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405706-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The initial CV curves of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode at 0.2 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) The initial GCD curves of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Rate performance of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene, pure CoSe<sub>2</sub>, and CoS@MXene electrodes. d) Cyclic performance at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and e) corresponding differential median (d<i>Q/</i>d<i>V</i>) curves from 1st to 50th. f) Long-term cycling stability of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene, pure CoSe<sub>2</sub>, and CoS@MXene electrodes at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 10&nbsp;000 cycles. g) Comparison of the comprehensive properties between CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene and reported selenide-based or sulfide-based SIBs anodes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0003">3d</a> shows the cycling performance of the three samples at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Notably, the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode retains a reversible capacity of 694 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 125% after 100 cycles, which is significantly superior to that of pure CoSe<sub>2</sub> (657 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 120%) and CoS@MXene (504 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 92%) electrodes. The increased capacity is related to electrode activation and structural reorganisation. The detailed voltage plateau changes are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0003">3e</a>. The oxidation peaks in the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curve of three electrodes changed gradually with cycling and eventually stabilized. For CoSe<sub>2</sub>, the Cu-related oxidation peak (≈1.5 V) gradually appear from the 2nd cycle, whereas for CoS they appeared after 15 cycles. This indicates that selenides are more likely than sulfides to induce Cu conversion. Compared to pure CoSe<sub>2</sub>, CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene composite exhibit faster and stronger voltage change behavior. The interface construction between CoSe<sub>2</sub> and MXene can better achieve electrode activation and phase reorganization, while also possessing rapid Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.</p>
            
            <p>To investigate the effect of residual sulfur on electrochemical performance, we synthesized sulfur-free pure cobalt selenide and MXene composites (pure CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene). The morphology of the synthesized pure CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene sample is similar to that of the sulfur-containing sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a> (Supporting Information), pure CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene only achieve reversible capacities of 577 and 156 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.1 and 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which are lower than those of the sulfur-containing CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene samples. Furthermore, the initial GCD and d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves of pure CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene show similar potential changes to those of the sulfur-containing CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b-d</a>, Supporting Information). These results suggest that residual sulfur does not affect the copper-driven conversion reaction but rather enhances the electrochemical performance of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode.</p>
            
            <p>The long-term cycling capability of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene, pure CoSe<sub>2</sub>, and CoS@MXene electrodes was evaluated at a high current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0003">3f</a>). CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene delivered a high specific capacity of 437 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 10&nbsp;000 cycles with a capacity retention of ∼86% and a 0.0014% capacity decay per cycle, which is much higher than its counterparts of pure CoSe<sub>2</sub> (377 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 4000 cycles with a capacity retention of ∼77%) and CoS@MXene (136 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 10&nbsp;000 cycles with a capacity retention of ∼43%). The reported C@FeSe<sub>2</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub>@V<sub>4</sub>C<sub>3</sub> MXene composite has achieved 15&nbsp;000 cycles, but its capacity retention and capacity decay rates are only 72% and 0.0019%, respectively, and its capacity and rate performance are also relatively inferior.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0015_R_d177245273e1326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, as summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), most of the reported cycling numbers for selenide-based or sulfide-based anodes in the literature were less than 10&nbsp;000, with a capacity decay rate exceeding 0.001%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0007_R_d177245273e1335" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0008_R_d177245273e1335" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0012_R_d177245273e1335" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0014_R_d177245273e1335" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0020_R_d177245273e1335" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Lett.">20</a>, <a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0025_R_d177245273e1335" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Mater.">25</a>, <a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0031_R_d177245273e1335" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Mater.">31</a>, <a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0039_R_d177245273e1335" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">39</a>-<a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0050_R_d177245273e1335" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - ACS Nano.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although the reported FePS<sub>3</sub>/Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> composite exhibits higher capacity and rate performance (598 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>), its cycling numbers and ICE are only 2000 and 78% respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0030_R_d177245273e1353" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After comprehensive comparison, our CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene composite exhibits superior overall performance, as confirmed by the radar plot in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0003">3g</a>, where the parameter values of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene are located on the periphery.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">To explore the Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode before cycling and after 100 cycles, the CV curves were collected at different scan rates. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0004">4a</a>, the CV curves of the initial CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode show a similar and amplified shape with increasing scan rate, while the cathodic peaks were slightly shifted towards lower potentials and the anodic peaks were slightly shifted towards higher potentials. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0004">4d</a>, the CV curves after 100 cycles exhibited a quite different view compared to the original ones (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0004">4a</a>), with a series of changes in the peak positions and intensities, which is highly consistent with the sodiation/desodiation process of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0022_R_d177245273e1391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0023_R_d177245273e1391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Small.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the post-cycling CV curves exhibit smaller peak position shifts with increasing scanning rate, indicating weaker polarisation after electrode activation and structural reorganization. Peak current (<i>i</i>) increases with the increase of scanning rate (<i>v</i>), showing obvious capacitive behavior. This charge storage mechanism can be analyzed through the relationship between <i>i</i> and ν:
               <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202405706-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/1c4793a6-c2b0-4caf-961a-46618c7e8b19/aenm202405706-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202405706:aenm202405706-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/aenm202405706-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
                              <mspace width="0.16em"></mspace>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                              </mrow>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>ν</mi>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">b</mi>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{i}}\,{\mathrm{ = a}}{{{{\nu}}}^{\mathrm{b}}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where index b plays a crucial role in evaluating the sodiation/desodiation kinetic behavior and can be obtained through the log (<i>i</i>)-log (<i>v</i>) curve. When the <i>b</i> value approaches 0.5, the storage behavior is limited by diffusion-controlled process from faradaic intercalation. As <i>b</i> approaches 1.0, it indicates surface-dominated process from capacitive charge storage. Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0004">4b,e</a> show the log (<i>i</i>)-log (<i>v</i>) plots of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode before and after cycling. The <i>b</i> values of typical cathodic/anodic peaks were in the range of 0.6-1.0, indicating the charge storage process of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene is jointly controlled by diffusion and capacitive behavior. The cycled electrode has a higher b value compared to the initial electrode, demonstrating its greater capacitive contributions. The post-cycle CoSe<sub>2</sub> and CoS@MXene electrodes also exhibit increased b values (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that active components related to Cu can be introduced during the cycling process, increasing the active sites of the electrodes and accelerating the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption. In addition, the capacitive contribution ratios can be accurately determined through the equation:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202405706-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/aa1100c2-23cf-4abd-9897-b55f7d01f6d5/aenm202405706-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202405706:aenm202405706-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/aenm202405706-math-0002.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>

                              </mfenced>

                              <msub>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">k</mi>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">v</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">k</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">v</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                    <mo>/</mo>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{i}}\left( {\mathrm{V}} \right) = {{{\mathrm{k}}}_{\mathrm{1}}}{\mathrm{v}} + {{{\mathrm{k}}}_{\mathrm{2}}}{{{\mathrm{v}}}^{{\mathrm{1/2}}}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>where <i>i</i>(V), k<sub>1</sub><i>v</i>, and k<sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> stand for the current at a given potential, capacitive current, and diffusion-dominated current, respectively. The surface-dominated capacitive contribution for the pristine CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene, CoSe<sub>2</sub> and CoS@MXene electrodes at 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup> are 86%, 85%, and 82%, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0004">4c</a> shows the capacitive contribution ratios of the three electrodes at different scan rates, where the capacitive contribution of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode is significantly superior to the other two electrodes with increasing scan rate, consistent with its outstanding rate and cycle characteristics. In addition, the capacitive contribution ratios of the three electrodes after cycling have been further improved at all scan rates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0004">4f</a>), indicating enhanced reaction kinetics of the cycled electrodes.</div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405706-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f9eadaeb-5831-465a-a1a3-7caea25660d3/aenm202405706-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f9eadaeb-5831-465a-a1a3-7caea25660d3/aenm202405706-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d1005359-dee4-4fda-b28d-442f2c89d89c/aenm202405706-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f9eadaeb-5831-465a-a1a3-7caea25660d3/aenm202405706-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405706-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">CV curves of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode a) before cycling and d) after 100 cycles at different scan rates. b, e) Linear fitting plots of log (peak current) vs log (scan rate) according to the CV curves of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode. Capacitive contribution ratio of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode c) before cycling and f) after 100 cycles at different sweep rates. g) EIS plots of different electrodes after 100 cycles. h) GITT curves and the corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) of different electrodes after 100 cycles. i) Average <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> during the discharging and charging process.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">To further explore the electrochemical kinetics between different samples, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests were performed on the electrodes. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0004">4g</a> shows the Nyquist plots for the three electrodes after 100 cycles. All the Nyquist plots show a semicircle in the high-frequency region corresponding to the resistance of contact parameters (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>) and charge transfer (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), and a sloping line in the low-frequency region related to the Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>) associated with Na<sup>+</sup> difusion in the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0051_R_d177245273e1573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Obviously, the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode displays smaller semicircle diameter (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> ≈14 Ω) than that of CoS@MXene (17 Ω) and CoSe<sub>2</sub> (36 Ω), indicating that the combination with MXene nanosheets is beneficial for enhancing charge transfer kinetics. Furthermore, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) can be analyzed and calculated using GITT. The GITT data was collected with a current pulse duration of 10 min and relaxation time of 30 min at 0.01 A g<sup>−1</sup> during the 100<sup>th</sup> cycle. The <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> can be calculated based on Fick's second law with the following simplified equation:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0052_R_d177245273e1599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>
               
               <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202405706-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/edf98dcf-598c-438c-8b63-257da2cb9cdb/aenm202405706-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202405706:aenm202405706-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/aenm202405706-math-0003.png">
