                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where <i>M</i><sub>B</sub>, <i>m</i><sub>B</sub>, and <i>V<sub>M</sub></i> are molar mass, mass, and molar volume of the active material, respectively. <i>τ</i>, <i>S</i>, Δ<i>E<sub>S</sub></i>, and Δ<i>E<sub>τ</sub></i> represent pulse duration, electrode area, and steady-state voltage change due to the current pulse, as well as potential change during the constant current pulse after eliminating the <i>iR</i> drop, respectively. As expected, the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode exhibits a higher <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> throughout the charging and discharging process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0004">4h</a>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0004">4i</a>, the average <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> during the discharging and charging process for the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode was calculated to be 12 × 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which is higher than that of CoS@MXene (2.6 × 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) and CoSe<sub>2</sub> (2.1 × 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>). The superiority of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode in Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetic enables the electrode to achieve fast-charge capability and withstand extremely long cycle life while maintaining the mechanical integrity of the electrode.</div>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405706-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405706-sec-0050-title">2.3 Na<sup>+</sup> Storage Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>To explore the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism and phase evolution of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode during GCD process, ex situ XRD, XPS, and TEM characterizations were performed. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0005">5a</a> shows the first and 25th GCD curves and the corresponding ex situ XRD patterns are shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0005">5b,c</a>. The first and 25th cycles exhibit different GCD voltage platforms, which also indicate different Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms. Firstly, two diffraction peaks at 21.7° and 25.3° were clearly observed throughout the entire charging and discharging process, corresponding to the (111) and (200) planes of the Cu collector, respectively. The peaks located at 30.9°, 34.7°, 36.3°, and 47.9° in the initial state were from CoSe<sub>2</sub>. During the discharge from the open circuit voltage to 0.98 V, the signals of the CoSe<sub>2</sub> gradually weakened. New peaks emerged at 22.9°, 25.0°, 26.4°, 31.8°, and 38.9° belong to the NaSe<sub>2</sub> (202), Na<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>2</sub> (121), Na<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>2</sub> (130), Na<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>2</sub> (211), and NaSe<sub>2</sub> (323) planes, respectively. They indicate the occurrence of intercalation and conversion reactions between CoSe<sub>2</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup>. Another two peaks at 29.6° and 33.0° were belong to the (200) and (210) planes of (Cu,Co)Se<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No. 25–0309). As the cell was further discharged to 0.3 V, the diffraction peaks of the Na<sub>2</sub>CoSe<sub>2</sub>, NaSe<sub>2</sub>, and (Cu,Co)Se<sub>2</sub> completely disappear with the appearance of Na<sub>2</sub>Se (JCPDS No. 47–1699) and Na<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No. 89–2754), which indicates that the conversion reaction has been completed. In addition, the absence of Co diffraction peaks may be due to its small particle size and low crystallinity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0053_R_d177245273e1811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Nat. Commun.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Reversible phase changes were demonstrated during subsequent charging, but the diffraction peaks of CoSe<sub>2</sub> can hardly be detected when charging up to 3.0 V, indicating the low crystallinity nature in the reconstructed sample.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0054_R_d177245273e1819" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Energy Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0005">5c</a> shows the ex situ XRD patterns of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene after stabilization for 25 cycles. Notably, the diffraction peaks of CoSe<sub>2</sub> disappeared, replaced by that of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se (JCPDS No. 88–2043), further demonstrating the conversion reaction between Cu current collector and CoSe<sub>2</sub>. The electrochemical driving force of this conversion behavior can be explained by the HSAB theory.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0022_R_d177245273e1836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As researched by Yin et&nbsp;al., the absence of diffraction peaks for elemental Co should be attributed to the fact that the majority of the replaced Co exists in ionic form after the transition behavior.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0027_R_d177245273e1841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When discharged to 0.3 V, the two characteristic peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>Se and Na<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>2</sub> can still be clearly detected, indicating the occurrence of the conversion reaction between Cu<sub>2</sub>Se and Na<sup>+</sup>. It is worth noting that some signals from Cu<sub>2</sub>Se are still detected. These incompletely converted Cu<sub>2</sub>Se, as a superionic conductor, will also be beneficial for accelerating the transport and storage of Na<sup>+</sup>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405706-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e84d05f6-4301-475e-abf6-632df2d85448/aenm202405706-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e84d05f6-4301-475e-abf6-632df2d85448/aenm202405706-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e017ff47-9870-4f92-ad66-307841e890f2/aenm202405706-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e84d05f6-4301-475e-abf6-632df2d85448/aenm202405706-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405706-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) GCD profiles of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene at the 1st and the 25th cycles under 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Ex situ XRD patterns of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene during b) the 1st and c) the 25th cycles. Ex situ XPS spectra of d) Cu LMM, e) Se 3d, and f) Co 2p for CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene after 25 cycles at fully discharged (D 0.3 V) and fully charged states (C 3.0 V). TEM images and EDS elemental mappings of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene after 25 cycles at g) D 0.3 V and h) C 3.0 V.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further verify the phase conversion of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se, the ex situ XPS was investigated for the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode after full cycles without Cu collector (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0005">5d-f</a>). In high-resolution Cu 2p spectra, the peaks centered at 955.0 eV (934.1 eV), 952.8 eV (932.7 eV), and 951.0 eV (931.2 eV) were indexed to the Cu<sup>2+</sup>, Cu<sup>+</sup>, and Cu<sup>0</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). Compared to the fully charged state (C 3V), there is a significant increase in Cu<sup>0</sup> in the sample under full discharge (D 0.3V), indicating the conversion of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se to Cu. To better distinguish between Cu<sup>+</sup> and Cu<sup>0</sup>, the LMM signal of Cu was measured, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0005">5d</a>. Obviously, Cu<sup>0</sup> is predominantly present in the fully discharged state, while Cu<sup>+</sup> (from Cu<sub>2</sub>Se) dominates in the fully charged state. The XPS peak analysis of Se 3d further indicated that interconversion between Cu<sub>2</sub>Se and Na<sub>2</sub>Se accompanied the charging and discharging process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0005">5e</a>). For high resolution of Co 2p (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0005">5f</a>), Co exists in the form of Co<sup>2+</sup> at the fully charged state. While at a discharged state of 0.3 V, the ratio of Co<sup>3+</sup> is significantly increased, indicating that Co gradually loses electrons, which is entirely opposite to the valence change of Cu. During this process, Co provides additional electrons to Cu<sub>2</sub>Se to accelerate its reduction reaction (Cu<sub>2</sub>Se + Na<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>−</sup> → Cu + Na<sub>2</sub>Se). This is because the formation of Co<sup>3+</sup> results in an electron-rich environment, which facilitates the reduction of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se. Conversely, Co will capture electrons from Cu to accelerate its oxidation reaction during charging. This is consistent with the research by Lei et&nbsp;al. on sodium-sulfur batteries, where Co can act as an efficient electron reservoir for catalytic sulfur redox reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0055_R_d177245273e1963" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This electron transfer mechanism is crucial for the overall electrochemical performance of the system.</p>
            
            <p>To further determine the impact of Co element on battery performance, a mixture of MXene and selenium powder was used as the electrode body. Co-free Cu<sub>2</sub>Se@MXene composites were prepared by electrochemical in situ driving and their electrochemical properties were detected in detail. The results are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). The electrode material without the Co element exhibited poor capacity and rate performance. The d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves during the battery cycling further demonstrated that a stable voltage potential related to Cu<sub>2</sub>Se was gradually formed under electrochemical in-situ driving, as indicated by the post-cycling CV curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16e</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, the pure Cu<sub>2</sub>Se without Co element showed a smaller b value, lower capacitive contribution and larger charge transfer resistance (20 Ω), indicating its poor electrochemical kinetics. These results indicate that the introduction of Co element can enhance the kinetics of the battery, thereby obtaining higher capacity and rate performance.</p>
            
            <p>In addition to Co elements serving as electron reservoirs, other transition metal elements, such as Ni, also exhibit similar effects. Here, NiSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene composites were prepared using the same synthesis strategy (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Electrochemical performance characterization (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17c–f</a>, Supporting Information) revealed that NiSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene composite also exhibit a Cu-driven conversion mechanism. However, its reversible capacity and rate performance are inferior to those of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene composite. Ex situ XPS characterization (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17g–i</a>, Supporting Information) also confirmed the existence of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se (Cu<sup>+</sup>) and that the Ni element can also act as electron reservoirs during the charging and discharging processes.</p>
            
            <p>HRTEM was used to further characterize the structural evolution of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene after cycling stability in fully charged/discharged states. When fully discharged to 0.3 V, two lattice spacings of 0.205 and 0.328 nm were observed, corresponding to the (311) plane of Na<sub>2</sub>Se and the (111) plane of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0005">5g</a>). After fully charged, only Cu<sub>2</sub>Se could be detected in HRTEM image, indicating the occurrence of the conversion reaction from Na<sub>2</sub>Se to Cu<sub>2</sub>Se (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0005">5h</a>). This result was consistent with the ex situ XRD results. In addition, their corresponding TEM mapping images show the homogeneous distribution of Co, Cu, and Se elements, further confirming the formation of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se. The atomic ratio of Cu and Co in the electrode after cycling was about 2.37:1 (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The in situ-formed Cu<sub>2</sub>Se will play dual roles as a new active material and a fast ionic conductor in the stabilized electrode, enhancing Na<sup>+</sup> storage and transport. Morphological changes on the surface of the Cu collector before and after electrochemical cycling also provide further evidence of the participation of the Cu element. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information), some corrosion pits emerge on the surface of the Cu current collector following cycling, resulting in a rougher and more uneven surface. Nevertheless, this has no impact on the long-cycle service life of the battery (as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0003">3f</a>). This could potentially be attributed to the fact that the degree of surface change is within an acceptable range.</p>
            
            <p>We should note that the performance enhancement of TMSe can only be achieved using Cu current collectors and ether-based electrolytes. When Al current collectors or ester-based electrolytes are used, the battery capacity rapidly decays (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19-S20</a>, Supporting Information). This is consistent with our previous research on Se electrodes and further demonstrates the promoting effect of ether-based electrolytes on the Cu-driven conversion process and the improvement of sodium storage performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405706-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405706-bib-0024_R_d177245273e2056" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is noteworthy that the Cu-driven conversion reaction also occurs when using different ether-based electrolytes (e.g., 1M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME), resulting in the attainment of stable capacity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405706-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405706-sec-0060-title">2.4 Theoretical Computations</h3>
            
            <p>To better understand the key role of active material reconstruction in enhancing Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance under Cu-driven electrochemical replacement process, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were employed. <b>Figures</b> <a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0006">6a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information) show the side-view and top-view structural models of Na adsorption on CoSe<sub>2</sub>, CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene, and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se@MXene. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0006">6b</a>, the Na adsorption energy of CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene was -1.11 eV, obviously lower than that of pristine CoSe<sub>2</sub> (-0.83 eV), indicating that the heterojunction can promote the Na adsorption and provide more abundant active reaction sites. Furthermore, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se@MXene exhibits more negative Na adsorption energy (-1.74 eV), indicating that the Cu-driven conversion reaction is more favorable for capturing and storing sodium ions. The robust Na adsorption capability of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se@MXene provides advantages for enhancing the Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity. To learn more about the reaction kinetics of the above three materials, the minimum energy paths and the corresponding diffusion barrier energies were investigated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0006">6c,d</a>, and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). As expected, the sodium ion diffusion energy barrier for Cu<sub>2</sub>Se@MXene was only 0.17 eV, significantly lower than that of CoSe<sub>2</sub> (0.39 eV) and CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene (0.26 eV). This finding suggests that the incorporation of MXene and the in situ conversion of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se together promote the sodium diffusion kinetics. Therefore, the significantly enhanced sodium adsorption energy and reduced sodium diffusion energy barrier jointly endow the Cu-driven reconstructed Cu<sub>2</sub>Se@MXene heterostructure with superior rate performance and cycling stability. Based on a series of ex situ characterizations and DFT calculations, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405706-fig-0006">6e</a> illustrates the Cu-driven electrochemical replacement process, the Co electron reservoir, and the corresponding sodium storage mechanism, as well as a profound comprehension of the excellent electrochemical performance of the reconstituted Cu<sub>2</sub>Se@MXene heterostructure.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405706-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/68e38911-43bf-471d-93c2-19b684696cf7/aenm202405706-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/68e38911-43bf-471d-93c2-19b684696cf7/aenm202405706-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6122fcd6-ff6f-4381-8c53-621c0223206d/aenm202405706-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/68e38911-43bf-471d-93c2-19b684696cf7/aenm202405706-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405706-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of Na atom adsorption on CoSe<sub>2</sub>, CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene, and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se@MXene and b) coresponding sodium adsorption energy. c) Side illustration of the diffusion paths for Na<sup>+</sup> on CoSe<sub>2</sub>, CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene, and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se@MXene and d) coresponding diffusion energy barriers. e) Schematic illustration of the Cu-driven electrochemical replacement process and Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism in Cu<sub>2</sub>Se@MXene.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405706-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405706-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>The aim of this study is to improve the capacity, structural stability and lifespan of SIBs by obtaining the final Cu<sub>2</sub>Se@MXene heterogeneous active material in a constructed CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene heterostructure prototype with a Cu-driven electrochemical replacement process. This heterostructure not only provides ultra-small active components but also offers a 2D conductive MXene framework, thereby realizing the full potential of active materials and an efficient electron/ion transport system. Ex situ characterizations confirm the Cu-driven electrochemical conversion mechanism and the corresponding sodium storage mechanism. Importantly, elemental Co can act as an electron reservoir, providing an additional supply of electrons for the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se conversion and accelerating its redox process. Furthermore, DFT calculations further reveal that the construction of the heterointerface and the Cu-driven conversion mechanism can significantly enhance the sodium ion adsorption and diffusion kinetics. Utilizing this superior structural and compositional reconstruction design, the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene heterostructure exhibits impressive rate performance and outstanding cycling stability as anode for NIBs. The CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene electrode achieves a high reversible specific capacity of 694 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Even at a high rate of 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the sodium storage-specific capacity of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene can still be up to 425 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Impressively, the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@MXene also delivers excellent long-term cycling stability (437 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 10&nbsp;000 cycles with a 0.0014% capacity decay per cycle). Our research not only elucidates the Cu-driven conversion behavior in metal selenides but also opens up new avenues for understanding the efficient sodium storage mechanisms in metal selenide and developing high-performance metal selenide@MXene composite anodes.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                  <li data-bib-id="aenm202405706-bib-0003"><span class="bullet">3</span><span class="author">Y. Gao</span>, <span class="author">Q. Yu</span>, <span class="author">H.
